Why Coding is The First Undeniable AI Use case | a16z

a16z(277.0K)·60:33·8.9K views·2026-06-08
Benedict EvansTech Analyst Erik TorenbergHost
TL;DR"Agentic coding went from being kind of useful to really changing…
While big tech's $700 billion AI capital expenditure boom mirrors past infrastructure cycles, agentic coding has emerged as AI’s first undeniable use case with true product-market fit. Long-term value will likely shift away from commoditized foundation models up to the...

TL;DR: While big tech's $700 billion AI capital expenditure boom mirrors past infrastructure cycles, agentic coding has emerged as AI’s first undeniable use case with true product-market fit. Long-term value will likely shift away from commoditized foundation models up to the application layer, dramatically expanding the volume of enterprise software.

Coding as AI’s First Breakout Use Case

  • Undeniable Product-Market Fit: Agentic coding has transitioned rapidly from a novel helper tool to an indispensable workflow driver. Unlike other speculative AI applications, developers are aggressively pulling coding agents into their daily workflows.
  • Diverging Vendor Strategies:
      • Anthropic: Leveraged its capital constraint relative to OpenAI by focusing narrowly on coding, successfully getting it to work first.
      • OpenAI: Attempted an "everything all at once" horizontal strategy before doubling down on coding once its high market demand became obvious.
  • Workforce Restructuring: The technology has reached a point where it is actively automating tasks previously assigned to junior engineers. However, the long-term career trajectory of software engineers and overall engineering team structures remain highly uncertain.

The Infrastructure Trap & Value Capture Lessons from History

  • The Telco Analogy: Benedict Evans compares the current AI landscape to the mobile data boom of 2009–2010.
      • Unprofitable Infrastructure: Since 2010, mobile data traffic has grown 1,500x to 2,000x, and telecom networks spend $200 billion annually in CapEx. Yet, telco stocks have remained flat for 20 years.
      • Value Accrual: Telecom companies failed to capture value because they built commodity infrastructure; the real financial upside moved up the stack to operating systems (iOS, Android) and applications.
  • Commoditization of Foundation Models: Evans argues that foundation models are not standalone products, nor are chatbots sustainable product interfaces.
      • Lack of Network Effects: Unlike Windows or iOS, foundation models do not inherently possess defensive network effects.
      • Margin Pressure: With multiple hyper-scale frontier model companies (spending between $200 billion and $2 trillion collectively) competing alongside robust open-source and edge models, model pricing will inevitably trend toward marginal cost.

The Future of Software & SaaS Economics

  • Explosion of Software Volume: Rather than consolidating the SaaS industry, AI will drastically lower the cost of software creation, leading to "way more software." Historically, SaaS gave businesses an order of magnitude more tools; AI will accelerate this fragmentation.
  • The Two Architectural Paths for AI Integration:
      • Bottom of the Stack (Features): AI functioning as a context-aware feature embedded directly inside existing deterministic databases (e.g., a "write email" button within Salesforce).
      • Top of the Stack (Orchestrators): AI sitting above existing enterprise tools (Salesforce, Workday, email, telemetry) to synthesize, analyze, and automate cross-platform workflows.
  • The Jevons Paradox in Action: Lowering the cost of computing, analysis, and software production will not result in lower overall spending. Instead, businesses will simply perform exponentially more analysis (e.g., running 50 financial models in 10 seconds instead of one over a week) for the same cost.

The Reality of Enterprise CapEx and Adoption

  • Limits of Financial Gravity: The big four tech giants (Microsoft, Meta, Google, Amazon) are on track to spend $700 billion on CapEx this year, with Microsoft, Meta, and Google spending 15% of their revenue on CapEx. This matches the capital intensity of the oil and gas or telecom industries and cannot scale indefinitely without clear ROI.
  • The "Los Angeles vs. San Francisco" Question: AI adoption questions are shifting from technological viability to industry-specific operations.
      • Domain-Specific Logic: Just as the critical questions for Netflix are entertainment business questions (not software engineering questions), the future of AI in law, consulting, or Hollywood will be decided by industry specialists who understand undocumented, implicit organizational processes.
  • From Automation to True Creation: Early-stage technology adoption always focuses on doing the "old thing faster" (e.g., writing emails or making PowerPoint slides). The real economic shifts will occur when organizations discover entirely new capabilities that were previously cost-prohibitive, such as hyper-personalized, visual e-commerce search or real-time predictive pricing based on multi-channel customer telemetry.

Notable Quotes

"Agentic coding went from being kind of useful to really changing everything... It’s going to be magic and in 20 years' time we'll just say, 'Well of course, how it is, computers have always done that.'" — Benedict Evans
"I don't think foundation models are a product, I don't think a chatbot is a product, I think the value will be further up." — Benedict Evans
"All software companies exist to solve problems created by other software companies... SaaS gave us an order of magnitude, two orders of magnitude more software, and we should probably expect that with this." — Benedict Evans
"There's a safe bet that Google, Meta, Amazon, Microsoft, Apple produced more profits than the entire telecoms industry... [The telcos] built this amazing piece of global, incredibly sophisticated, very expensive infrastructure... and they didn't make any money from it. Because all the value moved up the stack." — Benedict Evans

I think the third point here is—and, you know, there's a much more tangible observation—is that the pricing crunch that we've already mentioned looks to me a lot like what happened with mobile data in sort of 2009, '10. Where suddenly people got bills for like five, ten thousand dollars of data on the one side. And on the other hand, if you had flat-rate data, which is kind of what happened in the US with the iPhone—AT&T launches, AT&T Cingular launched the iPhone with flat-rate data, and then unfortunately everybody buys iPhones and they get 3G and people start watching YouTube, and the whole network goes down because they just don't have capacity to do that. It's funny, there are still people in tech who don't understand that cellular networks have marginal cost, and like, they have to add more capacity and that costs more money. But I think the other interesting part of that analogy is, or that comparison is, that since then mobile data traffic has risen by something like one and a half to two thousand times, and the mobile internet networks collectively have revenue of about a trillion dollars and they spend about 200 billion dollars a year on CapEx, and the stocks have been flat for 20 years, and all the cool stuff got built by somebody else. And they kind of all thought that they were going to build all the cool stuff. But that's kind of the point: they built this amazing piece of global, incredibly sophisticated, very expensive global infrastructure with enormous growth in use all the time, and it changed all of our lives and we all pay for it, and they didn't make any money from it. Because all the value moved up the stack. And this is of course absolutely, as I said earlier, this is one... like, absolutely central question for LLMs: can the model do the whole thing? Or do you have to have 300 apps built on top of it? Can you just go to the model and say, "Do my taxes for me?" Or do you need to have a tax thing that uses AI in 10 different ways inside it? And if not, then what is it to be a foundation model provider? Is this just commodity infrastructure that gets sold at marginal cost? Which somehow seems to be a very difficult concept for people to grasp right now because you can sell all the tokens you can make, so you can price it at ROI. But over the next couple of years we've got like a trillion or two trillion dollars of CapEx coming down the pipe, and the models get 100x, 200x more efficient every year, and then there's new models, and will the models use more tokens or less tokens? But we'll... like, we'll get to a different equilibrium, and why would that equilibrium be one where the model companies have pricing power when the models are all kind of the same, doing kind of the same thing with the same chips? Why would they have pricing power? And I think that... sorry, it's a long answer to your question, but, you know, you can go back and look over time: chip companies didn't capture the value. ISPs didn't capture the value. Mobile network operators didn't capture the value. Windows and iOS did, but they were doing something else—they had all these levers to go up the stack, and of course they had network effects, which models don't have. So that's sort of the question: do they end up like the infrastructure layers or do they end up like the operating system layers and capture value, and actually get to decide what gets built? Or do they end up... I mean, the irony there, of course, is Netscape, where Marc Andreessen famously said that he was going to turn Windows into a set of badly debugged device drivers, and then Microsoft kind of crowbarred their way into the market, but it turned out that web browsers weren't the point because all the value was somewhere else. And so I think that's kind of the more... these kind of swirling mass of questions around how this settles out. Which comes back right through to all of my kind of answer to your question: like, you know some of this stuff, but you don't know how it's going to work. Yeah. Yeah, it's unclear whether it looks more like the, you know, internet or sort of, you know, software where a lot of the value or just better margins happen at the application layer, or sort of the cloud where it seems, you know, where it sort of existed at the hardware layer. And right now so far it seems like Nvidia and going, you know, up, it seems like they have better margins and are accruing a lot of the value, but it's unclear if that will remain the same or if there will be sort of application, you know, if we'll look more like the internet. How... how would you even begin to predict such, you know, the answer to such? Well, so two answers to this. I mean, there's all these sorts of quotes about how history works and, you know, my favorite one is: history teaches us nothing except that something will happen. And, you know, you can always ex post facto say, "Well of course it worked out like that," but it generally wasn't obvious at the time. And you know, particularly I remember, you know, like sort of 15 years ago a lot of really, really clever people in tech looked at the iPhone and Android and said, "You know, this is open versus closed again, and Android is just going to crush the iPhone," which of course isn't what happened, and then I can go and explain why. But, you know, all of these... all of these comparisons are useful; none of them are predictive. And you know, it's always obvious in hindsight. I, you know, it's funny, I've... I've done a couple of podcasts recently and I've published this presentation, and there's like... there's a... there's a class of comment on this stuff which is to say, "You know, Benedict, you're not doing your job. You're supposed to tell us what's going to happen, you're supposed to make predictions, and all you seem to do is say, well, we don't know." And there's kind of two problems with that. One of them is there's a bunch of places where I actually do say, like, "I don't think this is going to work, I think it's going to work like that." Like, I don't think foundation models are a product. I don't think a chatbot is a product; I think the value will be further up. But the other side of this is like, when you're at this stage in the cycle, there's... there's... there's many paths and you don't know which of those paths it's going to be. And to try and say, "Well, I think it's going to be that one," is... you know, you might be right, but you do have to kind of be conscious of like how uncertain this is and how many different paths it could take. That's the nature of this part of the cycle: all the bets are open. You know, we get to the point where the S-curve kind of curves up and it narrows in and, you know, there was a moment when, you know, Windows Phone might have worked. In hindsight, no, probably it wasn't going to work. But, you know, there was a moment when it wasn't clear how mobile was going to work, and then there was a moment when it was clear, right, this is what's happening, now we move on to the next question, the next question. One of the characteristics of tech is that the moment that you understand something and you know how it works and what's going to happen is the moment you should move on to something else. You should always be looking for the places where we don't know what the answers are. Because, you know, I haven't updated my Apple spreadsheet in like five years because we know what happened: they won. Like, I don't care what the next year's... what... what this year's iPhone looks like, I don't pay attention to their market share in China. Like, it happened, next question. You mentioned the prediction of you don't think foundation models are... are the product, you think it'll move up. Explain that... the reasoning there a bit and what that could look like? So, I think there's like three or four, like, building blocks you can put on the table. One of them is that it's not clear how you could build a model that was fundamentally better than everybody else's model in some sort of sustainable differentiated way. There isn't... doesn't seem to be a network effect. There doesn't seem to be sort of levers you can pull and a strategy... a position you can get into where Instagram is, or YouTube is, or Google search is. Now, we don't see an equivalent of that for LLMs. Now, you have different emphasis—you know, this... maybe this one's better than that, maybe you like this one more than that—but there doesn't seem to be a sort of fundamental differentiation, fundamental competitive difference between the models except your willingness to spend money. The second problem is the chatbot itself is like a kind of a weird, limited V1 UI. And there's some things and some people and some kind of tasks where it works really well, but there are most of the others you need a bunch of other stuff. You need tooling, and it needs to be set up right, and it needs to have the right data, and it needs to be configured and controlled and have the right user interface, and people need to have kind of sat down and thought about how it should work. Because generally the people who are good at using the tool and doing the job that needs the tool are not the same people who are good at deciding what the tool should be. So, you know, people who are really, really good at designing print publications are not the people who should create InDesign. That's a different set of skills. And, you know, people who are really, really good at doing financial advice are not the right people to design TurboTax. These are different people with different skills. So, you have kind of groping around the middle of this. So you have, you know, Claude for this, Claude for that, and you have skills and so on. To me this is kind of like, well, one question is: well, who builds the skill? Another question is like, well, you know, that seems to be a bit like what you get if you do File New in Excel. Like, these are templates, and they'll take you so far, but at a certain point, you know, people outgrow the templates. There's a slide in my presentation which is a quote that somebody said to me on Twitter years ago. He said they were a consultant, and half of their jobs were telling people to use Excel to use a database, and the other half were telling people to use a database to use Excel. So there's this kind of fuzzy, swirly place of like, do you need dedicated software? Do you need horizontal software? Do you need vertical software? But you don't just do everything in Excel. There's al... you know, we've all seen the department that runs along on a 10-meg Excel file. Now, I run my business in Numbers, but on a spreadsheet, but like, there's a certain point where like you outgrow that. And so, following that on, well, can the model labs build all of that? Well, of course not, no more than, like, Microsoft or Apple could build every Windows app or every iPhone app. So then, do the model labs have leverage? Are they... are they Windows, or are they iOS? And again, well, is there a network effect? Like if you're a law firm right now and you buy a piece of software, like, you know, do the custom... are all the pieces of enterprise software that a16z has invested in, how often does, like, the law firm or the manufacturing company or the bank say, "Well, let's just use Claude, or let's use Open... or OpenAI because we've... we've standardized on Claude"? Well, no, that's not how it works. Any more than it worked like that for Cloud. Like, you didn't say, "Well, our company standardized on AWS." Like, you don't even know what cloud that SaaS product runs on. That's the whole point: it's abstracted away, it's not your problem. And so the foundation models seem to look more like that. They seem to look more like the hyperscalers in that sense, in that they don't have leverage. You know, they might have competitive advantages, but further up the stack you don't have leverage, you don't have a network effect, you don't have control. That sort of prompts me, incidentally, to say, well, maybe the right comparison here is with semiconductors, where with each generation it just gets more expensive, and so you have fewer players. So all of that kind of taken together, well, the models are kind of commodities, and the chatbot isn't the right UI or the right product, and the companies aren't going to be able to build all of that stuff themselves—so therefore, they're low-level infrastructure. And so then, well, do they have pricing power? Well, you're going to have—pick a number—three to six companies making a frontier model spending, no one knows, no one honestly knows, like something between 200 billion dollars and two trillion dollars a year on building these models, plus there'll be a bunch of edge and a bunch of open-source. So where's this going to settle down? You're going to have, as it might be, half a dozen companies that are all competing to sell this stuff. And so where is the price discipline going to come from, particularly when some of them have got, like, whole other business models as well, like, you know, Google selling ads? So, you know, they've got a different attitude to pricing than OpenAI. And so, like, I think the... the challenge here is like there's a difference between where we are right now and where this should end up. Which is kind of a first-year economics student kind of conversation: like, right now we're in this period of extreme dis-equilibrium of supply and demand and price and CapEx and capacity. But just because demand for tokens is infinite, that doesn't mean that you can't get to a different price equilibrium. Because of course that's what happened with mobile data: like, demand for this is infinite, it's grown 1500, 2000x in the last 15 years, but you still got to a supply and demand price equilibrium, and you still got a murderous price war between telcos in most parts of the world. Because fundamentally you're selling kind of a commodity to people who will swap back and forth, and of course developers will also swap back and forth. Now, this is, you know, I'm happy to say that this might be completely wrong. You know, maybe that we... we... we get to a world in which there's only two companies that can make an LLM and they have pricing power. Or we get to a world in which most of what we do gets subsumed into the model or they have leverage further up the stack. And it's kind of my point about iOS versus Android: just because you can say, "Well, it worked like that the last three times," that doesn't prove that it's going to... prove what's going to happen this time. But it does mean at least you should sort of ask the questions, and you should certainly at least say, as a sort of a primary observation, like, "This situation right now is transitory." You know, we're in this extreme scarcity, and then we have a pricing system, and we have a free market, and we have a surge of CapEx and, like, a trillion dollars of CapEx. So, like, those multiples are going to move around. And then what? Going back to... that's a good segue to your point you made earlier of, "Hey, you know, we know, you know, Apple's... you know, Apple won, next question." To... as a segue, what are some of the next questions that you're most focused on or that, you know, we should be paying the most attention to? So, I think one way to answer... so there's some of the questions we've already talked about, like, "Well, how far down the stack do the models go? Can the models differentiate?" and so on. I think another is obviously: at what point do we see more and more classes of use case where the models are good enough and we don't need the most expensive, fastest, biggest, heaviest model in the cloud? And you can use an older model, you can use an open-source model, you can have a model running on device, which obviously this is what Apple's going to be talking about in a couple of weeks—you know, how much can you push onto the device where the compute is free, or free to you anyway, and doesn't have marginal cost for the developer? I think another class of question is: it's almost like the questions move out of technology. So if you're looking at a law firm or a consultancy, or an investment bank or basically anyone in professional services where you traditionally had this pyramid structure and you can automate a great chunk of what the people at the bottom of the pyramid are doing, what happens? And the only thing you can say there is: if you have never worked at a law firm or never worked at Bain, BCG, McKinsey, you're probably not going to have a good idea of how this works. Because you probably don't really know what it is that all those associates are doing, and you also don't really know what it is that the client's paying for them. And how do those things kind of get reconfigured? And so, like, well, what does AI mean for finance? What does this mean for finance, both for how that internal like hiring structure and the kind of products you can create and, like, the margin structure? What does it mean for consultants? What does it mean for the Big Four, for the Big Three, for Accenture, for big law firms? And for advertising. And you can probably know some of those questions, but if you're not kind of in that industry, you don't really know the answers. The thing this reminds me a lot of is... is something I wrote when I was at a16z, which I called "Content Isn't King." And I also wrote something that said Netflix isn't a tech company. And the point I was kind of getting at is that if you look at Netflix, this whole thing is enabled by stuff that the tech industry has built. But all the questions for Netflix are TV, are LA questions. Like: what shows, how many shows, what kind of shows, what should you pay the talent, should you aim for awards, should you do movies, should you buy sports, what kind of sports? These are all Los Angeles questions; these aren't San Francisco questions—like, no one in San Francisco even knows what the right questions are. They're media industry questions. And this is... it was kind of my point that... that... that all the questions that matter for Netflix have become media industry questions. You can... this is obviously the great tension point about Tesla: is it a car company or is it a technology company? And so what I'm kind of getting at is that, you know, what does this stuff mean for law is kind of a question for lawyers, as much as it is or people who understand a lot about law firms and how they actually work and what they're actually doing and what the clients are actually buying from them. Same thing for, like, what does generative video mean for Hollywood? Like, you know, Ben Affleck probably knows a lot more about this than I do. Like, he built a company and sold it for, like, a hundred billion... hundred million dollars, so obviously he does, but like, you know. That... so that's kind of a second question, which is the questions move outside of AI and they become sort of half-AI questions, half something else kind of questions. And then the third level, which I think—and I probably should have said this earlier—the way that all of this is sort of fundamentally different from previous platform shifts is that with, you know, 3G or the iPhone or broadband, the web, or whatever it was, you didn't know what was going to happen next, but you knew the physical limits. Like, you know, 1995 you knew that telcos weren't going to give everybody in the world broadband next week, and you knew that everyone in the world wasn't going to go out and buy a PC because a PC cost like $3,000. So you kind of knew the basic physical limits of what... of what could and couldn't happen, and with generative AI obviously we don't. Like, we might like look at our phones when we get off this recording and there's a push notification that says that, like, OpenAI's new model is out and it's like 2% of the price because they worked something out. I mean, I don't think it's very likely at this stage, but like, we don't know those kinds of questions. So how much bigger will the models get, how much better, how much faster, how much cheaper, how much, you know, pick... or, you know, in what ways will the characters of the models change? We don't know. And that's different to previous platform shifts where you did know the sort of fundamental constraints. And so that will... that will kind of spin off new questions. And in a sense, this is something I pointed to earlier: I said, like, the place that's got product-market fit right now is coding. Nothing else has equivalent product-market fit right now. I don't, you know, I think I'm pretty safe in saying that, you know, Anthropic's gone from whatever it was, 9 billion run rate at the end of last year to 47 billion dollars run rate right now. If you had to guess, what are the use cases outside of coding that could potentially yield, you know, daily activity? Mm. So the... I should say, the sort of presentation that I published a couple of weeks ago, there are sort of three sections, and one of them is talking about capital and CapEx and infrastructure and foundation models and differentiation, which is the stuff we talked about. And the second is, well, how would you build software with this, and what does this do for the software industry, and what would software look like if you... and what does the margins, what happens to the margins of the companies and everything else? And the third section I called "Change," which is kind of getting to this point. And I opened it with, again, what appears to upset a certain category of person where I said, you know, the Yogi Berra quote that, you know, predictions are hard, especially about the future. And I think there's a sort of a back-test point, which is: imagine asking these kind of questions about the internet in 1997. What would you have got? What would you have not got? But I think one way you can look at this is to say, well, this is automation—that this makes a class of thing that people used to do that couldn't be automated, now you can automate that. And so then, well, what does that mean? And I propose, like, three or four buttons to press. First one is: is this just price elasticity? Which is really what the Jevons paradox is. Like, if you make it cheaper to do stuff, do you do the same amount of stuff for less money? Or do you do the same... more for the same money? Or do you do more for more money because it becomes so much cheaper? Do you... was there something that you couldn't do before that now becomes cheap? Was there something that was expensive and you were doing as a barrier to entry, like owning a printing press as a newspaper? Like, is there something that now was... was a barrier to entry and a cost-based... a barrier to entry that now goes away? Is there something that gets unlocked in your business model or in your competitive space because this thing became cheap? And then, like, the sort of the final question would be: like, what stuff was just completely impossible—like, cost... completely cost-prohibitive—that... so that nobody had even thought about it, and now that's within reach? And the example I used to give here was: like, well, steam engines make trains possible, and it wouldn't matter how many horses you buy, you couldn't have a train or like an express train. A much more contemporary example would be to point to something like YouTube or, indeed, point to Spotify. Like, you know, Spotify says, "You know, step one of the... look at the last 25 years of the music business." The first half is what happens if you don't have to buy a $15 CD to get that track, but then the second half is: what if $15 a month gets you all the music that there is, which is something that was just completely impossible. This is all... the problem... the problem with these kind of making predictions like this is that on the one hand, you're going to say stuff that's kind of clever and obvious, but... but like, you don't actually know what it's going to mean industry by industry. So like, if we'd been back in the late '90s and we'd said, "You know, the internet will destroy the value of physical distribution," it turned out that meant completely different things for newspapers and movie studios. Like, newspapers got completely screwed by this, and movie studios are kind of not really changed very much. So, again, it depends. The other kind of part of this is, you know, try... you can... there are some places where I think you kind of can, like, ask more useful questions. And the one that sort of intrigues me is to say, well, how does this change advertising and e-commerce and brands and marketing and everything that we buy? Just, you know, advertising is a trillion dollars and retail is 25 trillion dollars, so you know, it's a reasonable-sized TAM. So, the thing that I've always used to think about was that Google and Meta and Amazon don't really know what that product is. They know it's a SKU, they know what the publisher typed in the metadata field, and they know that people who bought this also bought that. But they don't really know why, and they don't really know what those things are—which is why you get these jokes about, you know, "Hey, Amazon, I bought a toilet seat cover. I'm not collecting toilet seats." Because Amazon doesn't really know what a toilet seat cover is and doesn't know that people don't buy two. Actually, they should know that; that should be frequency analysis, but they don't. And with an LLM, like, in principle you would kind of know what those things are and why people buy them and what other things people buy. And obviously, "know" is like a difficult, tricky term to use—what do you mean when you say know?—but at a minimum, a very different level of statistical correlation of... of what an AI system would be able to do. Which is, of course, why you see the ad numbers and the, you know, the conversion rates shooting up in every quarter from Google and Facebook. Because they're rolling all of this into their ad systems and their recommendation engines and their prediction algorithms. And like, they... you get shown more stuff that you would like, and the ads that you're shown are more likely to be things you'd like to buy, and so they have these... this enormous, this sudden acceleration in their ad revenue. All of which is... is to say, like, you kind of look at how these systems work and right now they say, well, "People who bought that could buy this." And you should now be able to say—like, the slide I had in the presentation was like, "Here's a picture of a coat, what is it, where can I buy that?" And like, five years ago that really wouldn't have... 10 years ago that certainly wouldn't have worked, five years ago that probably wouldn't work, now that should work. And then you can say, "Okay, suggest 10 other coats like that with different prices, and tell me where I can buy them, and suggest the pros and cons of each one," and you'll kind of get that too. And then you can push one step further and say, "Look at my Instagram and suggest a winter coat I should buy that will change my look but not too much." And again, like, three years ago that would have been total science fiction, and now you think, yeah, you could probably build something like that. That would kind of work. Those kinds of shifts in like what the computer knows, what it can automate, what suggestions it can make. The... going back right to the beginning, like, whenever you get a new technology, you start by doing the old thing but more. You know, more spreadsheets. Like, more PowerPoints, more... more email, better email. But the important stuff is not doing the old thing but more; it's doing something new that you couldn't have done with the old thing. I mean, that's a pretty banal observation, but you kind of lose sight of it. And so, what are the new things that you can only do with this, as opposed to automating the old stuff? I mean, I think the... the enterprise version of this would be: you know, you've got all our Zoom calls with clients recorded, and you've got all the flows of emails in and out of Salesforce, and you can see all the telemetry and the metrics and the analytics of how people use our product. So how should we change our prices to improve our churn? And again, that's something that an LLM might be able to do, which is very different to saying, "You know, do sentiment analysis on calls into the call center and tell me which customers are angry." You know, you get kind of multiple shifts in the layer of abstraction around what analysis you can do, and of course that then creates new companies and destroys old companies and creates new businesses and everything else. But again, we're in 1997 and I'm trying to predict Uber and Airbnb, and if I could actually do that... there's a sort of general point here, which is if we could actually predict what was going to happen, we'd live in a parallel universe. You know, VCs would have... it wouldn't be a one-in-ten hit rate; it would be a ten-out-of-ten hit rate. It... it seems like, yeah, one of the questions we're now asking is what... or sort of what follows is sort of: what was unreasonably expensive to do before that now is possible? And maybe, you know, is it some... something crazy like rebuilding YouTube from scratch or rewriting Linux from scratch or... Yeah, it's funny. I mean, the... you know, the other... the paired fallacy, of course, is the new thing comes along and says, "Well, we're going to build the new thing with the old thing... with the new thing." Of course, we're going to... we're going to build Office with open-source, we're going to rebuild it on the web, and, you know, it turns out like, you know, guess what, look at Google Docs—it's got like 20% of the market because that's actually not the point. What's interesting is to do something else, is to do something new, and it's to shift that level of abstraction and... and kind of spot problems that never existed. I mean, I... you know, it's the experience you get sitting in pitches all day at a venture firm is: there's some stuff where you think, "Well, that sound... sounds kind of useful," and the stuff where you think, "Mm, I'm not quite sure that... why that would work." But there are some things that like kind of fill a hole in the universe. And like, as soon as somebody explains it to you, you think, "Wow, why did never... nobody do that before? Why did no one see that that thing existed?" And that's, you know, where... you know, part of the fun part of looking at startups is, and that's what people will do with this. People will suddenly work out a way that you could turn... will work out that that problem existed, and no one, including the people who have that problem, no one realized that problem existed. And then they'll go out and make a thing to solve it. This is also, incidentally, go back to an earlier point, this is why I don't see the model... I think this is a problem with the idea the model will do the whole thing. And if you kind of... kind of go back and think about all the pitches you've seen since you joined a16z, how many of them were things where people in the industry knew that was a problem? Like, quite often the answer is actually no. Actually, no one in the industry thought that was a problem, and it was... actually took like two years to explain to them and persuade them that that problem actually existed at all, and that this new thing would fix that for them. That's kind of the problem with the idea that, you know, middle manager in finance is going to use this tool to solve this big global industry problem. Like, no, because no one knew that industry problem was there, let alone could... could work out the right way to build a tool to solve it. Does this imply a less consolidated SaaS environment than before AI? Maybe less bundling or single behemoths like the Microsoft enterprise? Gosh, what a way to bring me back down to earth. Like, "Is the SaaS industry going to be less consolidated, Benedict? That's all great, but tell us about the stocks." What are the kind of building blocks that we can put down here? So, obviously it's going to be way cheaper and quicker to build software. Obviously there's going to be a bunch of stuff you could do with software that you just couldn't do before at all. And so there will be more competition, there will... and of course this comes with a new margin structure, but as per our conversation earlier, we don't really know what that margin structure's going to look like. Are you going to go to, you know, outcome-based pricing? I think it's really hard to, like, tie each button press in a piece of enterprise software to P&L. Like, sometimes you can, like in Salesforce or something, but there's an awful lot of software it would be really hard to say, "Well, you know, the work I did today did this to the EPS, therefore this is what we should pay for it. This is what we should pay for that piece of software." I don't think that makes sense. Anyway, but what does the pricing structure look like over time versus now? There will be more competition, it will be easier to build stuff and quicker to build stuff. The way that I sort of thought about... I suppose this is... there's maybe kind of two framings to think about this that are kind of useful. One of them is to say that if you think about the sort of enterprise software fleet today, you've got like three buckets. You've got like your big iron horizontal systems, so SAP and Workday and your CRM and your capital management software and your payroll management software and so on. And then you've got vertical software, and typical big US company has like 3 to 400 SaaS apps, and then like another thousand apps that they built, bought, or built themselves internally running on-prem. And then in the middle you've got this kind of fuzzy, improvised space of Excel and email and the shared file system. And so, and stuff kind of moves back and forth between those, and like, in principle every SaaS app is doing something that you could have done in SAP or you could have done in Excel. Like, you could have, you know, managed your graduate recruiting in Workday, but at a certain point like... if you're—this was a friend's conversation the other day—like if you're PwC and you hire however many thousand graduates every year to train to be accountants, you've probably got a piece of dedicated software that you built for yourself, or maybe you hired Accenture to build, and you probably hate it, but anyway you've got this piece of dedicated hiring software, or you bought something. If you are a company that hires five graduates a year, you're doing that in email and a shared Google sheet because why would you buy software for that? And then like... then there's a space in the middle: do you do it in Workday, do you do it in Excel, do you do it in a dedicated app? And now you add ChatGPT to that. Do you do that in an LLM? Is there an LLM tool that means you can do that in Salesforce where you couldn't do it before, or you can do it in your vertical app that you couldn't do before? Do you use the LLM to build yourself a tool for that, just as you might have a company department that runs on a 10-meg Excel spreadsheet that someone built 15 years ago and no one knows how it works—no one knows how it works but they're still using that? So it kind of... it arrives within this kind of broad, fragmented, complicated landscape, and it's another set of options for how you would do that task. So that's kind of one framing to think about it. I think the other framing to think about this is: does the LLM go at the top of the stack or the bottom of the stack? So on the one hand, bottom of the stack is a feature inside Salesforce. So you're in Salesforce: "Look at the history with this customer, look at the context of every other sales call we've done, look at our business objectives, and suggest an email, or suggest to me what I should do here and what I should send, call to the customer." So it's a feature, it's a button that's controlled and has tooling and red guardrails and everything else that are driven by that particular use case. The other way to look at it is the example I gave earlier, which is, you know: "Go look at Salesforce and Workday and all of our email and Google Analytics, and then synthesize something that you couldn't have done before." So the... the tension in both cases is: where do you put the probabilistic software that can make mistakes, and where do you put the deterministic system software that can't answer these kind of questions? So where do you put the database and where do you put the LLM, and is it that kind of... which is at the top and which is at the bottom? And the answer is probably both, depending on... on... on what you're doing and where it goes. All of which is a long way of saying, like, what does this do to software? And the answer is more software, like way more software. I mean, all software companies exist to solve problems created by other software companies. I mean, that was a joke in security: like, all security software exists to solve problems created by other security software. And clearly, that's what we went through with SaaS. SaaS gave us an order of magnitude, two orders of magnitude more software, and we should probably expect that with this. What that gets you to is the SaaS apocalypse, where the investors are kind of looking at all these companies and saying, "Well, we don't really know which of these companies are going to get screwed by all of this, but some of them must be." Like, obviously, there must be some. You go through the end of this and, you know, X percent of all the SaaS companies that are out there are going to get wiped out by this. But you don't know which ones. So you probably shouldn't derate the whole thing by 50%, but clearly you're going to go, "Mm, I'm not sure I'm going to be long software at the moment until I have some idea of what the hell is going on." You said in your talk with Ben Thompson that software is someone sitting down, designing a workflow, and saying, "This is the right way of doing this from now on." But you also said that a process grows out of the way just a business runs. Does that just take time? Or do you think we need more experimentation and iteration from these vertical AI startups to get just the right shape of software for the future? Well, in a sense, I mean, maybe kind of an interesting turn on this is this is both what strategy consultants do and software companies do. They kind of look at what's going on inside a company and say, "Well, this is a crap way of doing it, and this would be a better way of doing it that would achieve your objectives better." A software company kind of encodes that in software. And, you know, a strategy consultancy kind of encodes that in workflows, org charts, processes, training, objectives, and, you know, maybe tells them to buy some software to do that thing, or maybe now increasingly builds them that software as well. I think another thing to talk about here is how much of what's done inside an organization is implicit, not documented, not in the training data, and not something that anybody in that company could actually sit down and draw you a neat flowchart of and explain to you. That is a big chunk of the value of Bain, BCG, McKinsey. They have license to come into a company and talk to everyone—and talk to the people you're not allowed to talk to that are in a different org—and not get fired. They go and work out how this actually works as opposed to how it's supposed to work, and why it is that people aren't doing the strategy—because actually, guess what, their bonus targets depend on them not doing the strategy. They work all of that out and are a team that's ready to come in from the outside and give you the answer, and then you can blame them or have that kind of pre-baked solution. Those are sort of problems in organizational management and how people function and how people can explain what they do. Those things are very hard to write down and very hard to kind of bake into a Claude skill and say, "There you are, like, make me a PowerPoint." And so there's a sort of broader "how does this always work" challenge here of how do you get people to use these technologies? How do you get people to adopt new tools? How do you work out how to help people adopt new tools and work out what new things you would do with them? This is also what happened with cloud, the web, mobile, the internet, PCs, spreadsheets, and so on. Do you think there's some kind of co-evolution between AI-native software and new types of interfaces? For example, new customer service AI platforms that might not have had as much human-facing UI, or system of record software being built without a front-end at all because its primary user will be AI agents querying it directly? So, I think these are kind of interesting ideas. They're things I struggle to have a strong opinion on because, you know, I'm not deep, deep into the weeds of how enterprise infrastructure gets bought. I wonder how new some of these questions are. I remember Chris Dixon saying, like, 10 or 15 years ago that APIs are the new BD and software companies could just open up their APIs. Well, what's old is new. You know, you don't need an API anymore; you just have an MCP server and people just plug—the agent will just plug into that. I don't know. I think the challenge with a lot of this stuff is that all the decisions are really exception handling. Like, the question is always: what can you not automate? What requires someone to make a decision and some judgment and have an opinion about it because maybe that hasn't been written down, or that didn't happen before, or doesn't look quite the way it happened before? I think there's a sort of, you know, various ways of kind of thinking about separating out what gets automated and what doesn't. The way that I used in the deck was to talk about what's a task versus what's a job. And often the tasks that are used to accomplish the job might change without the job itself changing very much, or without the thing that the job is selling to the client changing very much. Like, if you think about what accountants did 50 years ago and what accountants do today, they spend almost none of their time doing the same things, but to the client it's kind of the same thing. It just gets done in a completely different way with a whole bunch of different tasks. And one of the sort of more profound or abstract ways to think about this is: where is it that you want the average? Where is it that what you want is the way that everybody will do this? That's the way everyone would do it. That's what anyone would say. That's what anyone would make. That's what any associate would make. That's what anybody would give me. That's the answer anyone would give. Versus where is that not what you want? Where is it that you want the answer to a new question, or a different answer, or a different idea? Because LLMs are going to be very good at anything where you can describe how people do it and where what you want is the way anybody would do that. And they are not so good at where you can't really explain why you did it like that, and where you're doing it differently to the way people would normally do it. Various people, including, you know, the CEO of Google, have said that the risk of under-investing is riskier than over-investing. Is there any level of CAPEX where that stops being true, and are we getting there now? Well, there's a financial gravity problem in that Microsoft, Meta, and Google are all on track to spend 15% of revenue on CAPEX this year. And, you know, we think of telecoms as being capital-intensive; telecoms spend sort of 15% to 20% of revenue on CAPEX. So, you know, $700 billion is the guidance from the big four companies this year. Well, you know, telecoms is $300 billion, mobile is $200 billion, total telecoms is $300 billion. Oil and gas, depending on which definition and which bits of it you're counting, is anything from $700 billion to a trillion dollars, I think, from memory, depending exactly on who you ask. So $700 billion a year is like an impossibly large amount of money. It's—big global infrastructure costs just a lot of money. Clearly, like, those companies could not spend $1.5 trillion next year. Or if they did, they'd have to borrow it, and they certainly couldn't sustain that level of spending for any length of time. And so there's a certain point at which, like, that growth has to slow down because there isn't any more money. Now, clearly you can talk about ROI and your ability to produce returns from that investment. And, you know, clearly the capital markets are willing to fund that up to a point. But, like, pick a number at random; we can't spend $10 trillion a year on AI infrastructure because there isn't $10 trillion a year there to spend on it. So there's a finite—there are kind of like laws of physics caps on the amount of money that's available. I'd hesitate to say something more tangible than that at the moment. I mean, I'd kind of almost go back to what I said at the beginning, that we've got a multiple—we've got a bunch of multiples. So there's far more demand than supply. On the other hand, the efficiency is increasing massively. We don't know what the next model will be, and we don't know where edge or open source come in yet—when edge and open source come in yet. And meanwhile, you are always chasing the next model. And so this is kind of the line that runs across all of it: the model is only relevant for three to six months, six to nine months, whatever you want to say. And the model costs how many billion dollars? And how much infrastructure do you need to do that? I don't think that math is really shaken out yet. I mean, obviously you can—there's a bunch of very clever semiconductor analysts who spend lots of time trying to put numbers on this. It is kind of like trying to put numbers on bandwidth, internet bandwidth in the late '90s. Like, you kind of know what the rows in the spreadsheet are, but you don't really know where the values are. All you can really say is, well, look, it can't be—there's clearly physical limits on this. I think, you know, another way to answer the question is like if you're Google or Meta or Microsoft, to some extent Amazon, to some extent Apple, this is sort of an existential problem. And you have a sort of, you know, a FOMO problem in that, so, on the one hand the returns on the investment at the moment are hugely positive. On the other, you can't let other people get away with this without you participating because then your company's gone. And you don't want to end up like Microsoft in the 2000s or IBM in the '90s or indeed Meta in the 2010s where they are kind of continually getting shafted by Apple. So if this is the future of compute, then you need to be participating in it. But obviously at the same time the CFO is sitting there saying, "Well, yeah, that's great, but how much participation are we talking about here?" And I don't think we've—it's clearly at a certain point that curve is going to have to taper off because, like, there's nowhere else it can go. Do you think there's going to be a reckoning around token-maxing? Is it possible that companies have been overshooting AI usage, and when they do proper ROI studies, they'll pull back? Well, obviously, you know, you've had people like using the most expensive model to dicker around on the internet. Which is kind of what happened with mobile in, you know, 2010. Like, you know, you got a $10,000 bill and you said, "Wait, wait, I thought this was a flat rate bundle. Like, what happened?" So there's—you've obviously got a bunch of like silly slash painful stories. I think there's also a point of, like—I think maybe what's slightly more interesting as a question is, and clearly there's going to be a point in which, as I've said several times, we're at a moment of kind of massive disequilibrium. And the pricing has got to get back into alignment with the cost, and the usage has got to get into alignment with the pricing and the ROI. The challenge is it's a bit tricky. In this early stage, it's quite hard to know what the ROI is. It's rather like giving everybody the internet in the late '90s and saying, "Okay, go off, be more productive." And if you look at, like, there's a survey from Deloitte—there's also a survey from the Fed that's in my presentation—where if you go and ask CFOs, "Where have you seen the benefits?" most of the benefits so far have been stuff that's pretty hard to measure. So, like, better analytics, better customer support, more productivity—you can make more slides more quickly, you can do the analysis more quickly. It's kind of tough to put a financial value on that. It has a financial value, but it's not the same as saying, "Well, we made this new thing with AI and it had this revenue, or it saved this much money." Those things, just obviously, those things take longer. It's harder to build a new revenue line than to give this to everybody and have them use it to make spreadsheets more quickly. So there's a little bit of like, well, how long does this take? I think the other answer, of course—the problem here, of course, is consumer surplus, which is to say that this is kind of what happened with Excel. In that, you know, guess what? If a DCF takes you a week, then you probably only do one or two DCFs. And if a DCF takes you 10 seconds, then you do 50 DCFs. But you can't charge any more money for that. So some of what happens is that these things become competitive necessities and everybody has to buy it and use it, but the cost saving or the productivity gain that you get from it just kind of gets competed away. So you don't get to charge more for it. You know, I mean, if you're at McKinsey, Bain, or BCG, and doing that piece of analysis used to take a week and now it takes a day, you probably do five times more analysis and charge your customers the same. And like, your cost base hasn't changed either. So, you know, it's—which is exactly the way to think about what happened with investment banks and financial analysis. You just went and did way more analysis with probably fewer people and charged customers the same amount of money. A big part of your thesis is this idea that models are going to end up as commodities, and yet the layer that's raising the most money in the fastest time in history is these foundational model companies. So given that, what advice might you have for them, either collectively, or we can pick on someone individually, in order to adapt? It's not that I know that they're going to become commodities. My position is more now, "Well, like, okay, here is a chain of argument that says that deterministically it looks like these things will be commodities, and explain to me why they won't be." And that's as far as I would commit to that. I think, you know, the raising all this money—I kind of go back to my point about mobile, which again has very predictive value, but it's a worthwhile observation. The mobile industry is very big and spends a lot of money on infrastructure and isn't very profitable, and all the cool stuff is done by somebody else. And then you can do, you know, well, what's the return on capital? And the answer is, "Well, it depends on which market, whether you're in America or Europe or India or China." But meanwhile, like, that was a worthwhile thing to do and it produced a return for somebody, but then they ended up not controlling the whole thing, and other people ended up getting more value from that than they did. You know, I don't have the number in my head—what was Google's net income last year, was it what, $50 billion or something? What's the net income for, you know, the total telecoms industry? I should really subscribe to Bloomberg, then I could just answer these questions instantly. But like, it's a pretty safe bet that Google, Meta, Amazon, Microsoft, and Apple produced more profits than the entire telecoms industry. So this is a puzzle: you're driving the frontier forward, you're kind of caught in this trap that you have to keep competing because otherwise they'll do it and you'll fall behind. You've also got this thing that we haven't talked about at all which is, "You know, hey, aren't we just building AGI? Like, we're going to build God in a box," which some people do believe, although it's kind of hard to analyze. But maybe. So, you know, you're going to carry on building this stuff. But the practical question is, "Well, how do you get things that people want to use that aren't software, that aren't software development?" I mean, that's a good business; is that the only business? If you pick a number of how many hundreds of billions of dollars it is to make the software industry more productive—great, that's worth a trillion dollars, maybe. But then what? How do you expand this into the rest of the economy, into everybody else? This is why you get these conversations about partnering with private equity, partnering with the consultancies where, exactly as we've been discussing, guess what? It is actually quite hard to work out what to do with this stuff if you're actually running a real company. So you go to Bain, BCG, McKinsey, or Infosys and Cognizant and IBM and Accenture, or private equity shareholders. So there is this sort of sense of like on the one hand you're—sorry, sort of trying to work out the answer as I speak. But on the one hand you're building these bigger and bigger models and you kind of feel like you've got to keep doing it. But on the other hand, yes, but what are people doing with it? Why do most people look at ChatGPT and not really think of anything to do with it today? Last question: is there anything from the presentation that you want to make sure listeners leave with? The thing that I used last year and I used again is an IBM ad I found from the early '50s, which has got a picture of a sea of engineers all holding up slide rules. It's an IBM ad, and it says, "An IBM electronic calculator gives you 150 extra engineers." And that's like, how many pictures have you seen at a16z where that was the pitch? We should kind of remember that we go through these waves of these fundamental technology changes every 10 or 15 or 20 years. They're all amazing and change everything and are completely unlike anything that's happened before. And so AI is amazing and transformative and completely unlike anything that's happened before. Mobile was quite a big deal, too. And so was the internet, and so were PCs, and so was computing. Those were all also very big deals where it was hard to tell what was going to happen. And so we should sort of presume as a base case: "Okay, well, we're going to go through that again, and, you know, that will produce a bunch of things that are in people's lives, and it will put a bunch of people out of work, and there'll be a bunch of stuff that we're not very happy about, and there'll be a bunch of stuff that we all think is great." And then in 20 years' time, we'll kind of forget that there was a world when computers couldn't do that. I mean, here we are, we've been on this call for an hour and our computers didn't crash, and we're streaming HD video to each other, and it's like, "Well, of course that worked." In fact, I'm also doing it with my iPhone, so my iPhone is streaming to my Mac over Wi-Fi, streaming video here. And, like, it just works. It's magic, and we don't notice it anymore, and I think that's really my kind of one-line description of how all of this is going to end up. It's going to be magic, and in 20 years' time we'll just say, "Well, of course that's how it is; computers have always done that." Yeah, that's a great place to—great place to wrap. The presentation is called AI Eats the World, it's on Benedict Evans's website, and it is excellent. There's a lot more that we didn't get to, so definitely go check it out. Benedict, this has been a great conversation. Thanks so much for coming to the podcast. Thanks. Great to chat.

Anker / Steven Yang: Consumer Electronics Death & birth, The Third Category, Product Philosophy

Zhang Xiaojun Podcast(33.4K)·217:33·5.9K views·2026-06-08
Steven YangFounder and CEO, Anker Innovations Zhang XiaojunHost, Zhang Xiaojun Podcast
TL;DR"Technology will always encounter the limit of human perception.
Steven Yang outlines Anker’s transition from a "simple product" company to a "Third Category" organization, focusing on building long-term moats through localized AI, embodied intelligence, and a culture of first-principles thinking. User Value vs. Tech Buzzwords: Yang...

TL;DR: Steven Yang outlines Anker’s transition from a "simple product" company to a "Third Category" organization, focusing on building long-term moats through localized AI, embodied intelligence, and a culture of first-principles thinking.

AI Product Philosophy: Beyond "AI Native"

  • User Value vs. Tech Buzzwords: Yang argues that consumers do not care about "AI Native" or "IoT" labels; they care about better experiences. AI’s true value lies in making hardware "adaptive," where devices sense user needs and adjust themselves automatically (e.g., a microwave that suggests settings based on content).
  • Localized AI in Security: Anker’s security systems (ranked #2 globally) already utilize local large models.
      • Privacy Focus: Data is processed on a local base station rather than the cloud.
      • Natural Interaction: Users can ask the system natural language questions like, "Where is the cat?" or "Is my daughter home yet?"
  • Development Scale: Anker employs nearly 300 AI engineers. Their investment focuses on models ranging from millions to tens of billions of parameters rather than trillion-parameter LLMs.

The Three-Stage Robotics Roadmap

  • Evolution of Embodied Intelligence: Yang categorizes robotics into three distinct morphological stages:
      • Stage 1 (2D/2.5D): Planar movement, such as floor sweepers and lawnmowers (Anker's current core robotics business).
      • Stage 2 (3D Interaction): Spatial interaction, exemplified by robot dogs. Anker is developing a "guard dog" to bridge the gap between detection and deterrence in home security.
      • Stage 3 (Humanoid): The "crown jewel" of manipulation. Anker is currently in a pre-research/exploration phase, waiting for the technology stack to converge before full commercialization.
  • "Deep Sea" vs. "Shallow Sea" Strategy:
      • Deep Sea: Markets exceeding $50B (e.g., Smart Glasses, Humanoid Robots).
      • Strategic Choice: Anker avoids the Smart Glasses "Deep Sea" due to intense competition from phone manufacturers and model companies, choosing instead to focus on robotics where they can build a unique technology stack.

The Three Layers of the Corporate Moat

  • Layer 1: Leading Technology: Essential but subject to "diminishing marginal returns." Once technology reaches the limit of human perception (like Retina displays), the competitive advantage is eventually neutralized.
  • Layer 2: Brand and Trust: Building a brand is difficult and time-consuming; trust is easily lost. Yang notes that Anker’s brand strength allowed them to sell $10,000 home energy storage units online without physical showrooms.
  • Layer 3: Mission, Vision, and Values: The deepest moat. It is defined by a community of "creators" who believe in first-principles and extreme excellence. Yang believes a company’s longevity depends on its ability to attract and retain these specific types of people.

Organizational Evolution: From "Easy" to "Hard" Mode

  • The Transition: Anker started in "Easy Mode" with simple products like power banks. Moving into complex categories like robotics requires "Hard Mode," where the organization must excel in every link of the chain without making mistakes.
  • The "Third Category" Company: A platform that empowers creators to build multiple successful product categories across various countries, rather than a single-product "unicorn."
  • CEO’s Role and Routine: Yang intentionally limits his time in the office to under 8 hours to avoid micromanaging.
      • Focus: He spends his time on "abstraction"—reading about history, politics, and military strategy to find common patterns in productivity revolutions.
      • Philosophy: A Third Category company should run on its "system" and its "creators" rather than being pushed forward by the CEO's daily commands.

Notable Quotes

"Technology will always encounter the limit of human perception. Once you hit that ceiling, others will inevitably catch up. Your excess technical advantage will be smoothed out over a long enough timeframe." — Steven Yang
"A good product company must create real value for the customer. It is impossible to be a great company if your technology is advanced but the user feels it provides no value." — Steven Yang
"The ultimate moat is a group of people who truly believe in the mission, vision, and values—people who seek the essence of things and demand excellence." — Steven Yang

解决方案都还完全不同。 所以这个时候我们觉得可能还不是一个产品化的时候，它比较适合去预言。 而相反的话呢，今天你观察像狗这样的产品，就是二阶段的产品， 其实各家的形态都已经非常接近了。 嗯，所以从硬件上来讲，这个技术战已经比较收敛。 那么回到今天的模型阶段的话，大家的做法基本上也是非常的的一致，对吧？就是今天 Agent 的投在一个模型上面，所以我们觉得那这样的那个产品应该是可以进入产品化的阶段。 所以我们就从去年开始，就是有一个团队，我们在开发一条机器狗。 其实狗今天在我们的生活中到底扮演什么样的角色，提供什么样的价值？ 对吧？那当然今天狗是一个宠物，它给你提供情感的价值，但你发现狗最早进入家庭的时候，它其实起的是看家护院的作用，它提供的是功能价值。 对吧？那其实你发现今天的宠物狗很大程度上已经失去了当年的那个功能价值。 而我们自己做安防系统，那么我们在这个全球的几百万户期气家庭里面持续面对的一个问题就是说，你在家里边装满了安防的系统摄像头，对吧，然后包括报景的设备，但是今天如果有人闯入你家的时候，事实上你能做的事情是很少的。 那今天我们觉得当你有条看家狗，在今天有闯入者被这个安防系统侦测到了时候，这只狗可以去驱离这样的一个闯入者，实现了了一个用户价值的闭环。 对吧？就不仅是今天发现闯入者，而且实际上是能够驱离闯入者，让你家真的变得安全。 嗯，这样的一种产品听起来是不是有道理的？ 所以事实上我们去做用户研究也发现说，我们那些 77 的用户，然后大家今天有相当的比例愿意用相当的金额去买这样的一条家庭的看家狗。 换句话说这个产品其实在我们开始做的时候，我们确定它是能卖出去的，而且它是能卖出去很大量的量，因为它能创造特别明确的客户价值。 你听起来这个时代跟上一个时代你们做产品的方方面面也没有什么很大的区别，对吧？依然是定义市场的能力， 定义产品的能力。 我觉得任何一个时代，只要你叫产品公司，你就必须给客户创造价值。 不可能说你的产品对吧，技术特别先进，但是哦客户其实觉得它没有价值。 那这绝对不是一个好的产品公司。 所以我觉得我们这 30 多年从简单的产品，像充电宝那样的对吧，一直做到今天复杂的像看家狗这种产品。 公司的 我们信念非常的一贯，就是我们一定要做给客户创造真实价值的产品。 那为什么这个战略要从 前海转像深海呢？有转过型吗？ 我们还是说今天深不是今天这个品类有多复杂，而是代表这个品类的规模有多大。 对吧？换句话说，我们说所谓的深是这个品类的销售规模在 500 亿美金以上的。 这个其实它就像我们刚才举的德州扑克的例子。 实际上是你是这个德州扑克桌上每一住 盲住的大小。 所以换句话说就是我们说深海，就是盲注在几百万人民币对吧，到可能几千万人民币的市场的。 然后所谓深，就是一个盲注可能五个亿十个亿的市场。 所以在这样的市场里面，你发现其实即使是，对吧，就是有几百亿的钱甚至几百亿的钱，其实你可能打不了几注牌就会下去了。 所以那种市场我觉得其实跟—— 就我们说那个是深海和浅海的差别。 从这个角度的话，我们今天做的依然也是深海的市场。 你理解，对吧？即使是安防系统，像看家狗这种市场，它的整个市场规模大概率也不会超过 500 亿美金。 所以前海战略是依然延延的。 你回晋升海吗？ 其实你现在能看得到的两个未来确定性好出来的深海，一个就是我们刚刚讲的人型机器人。 对。 对吧？然后第二个呢其实我们觉得是智能眼降。 也就是个人交互终端的第——全新的这一种形态。 那今天人型眼降的话，我们觉得前面有一大堆公司。 这是智能眼降。 对，智能眼降。 还要我们觉得前面会有三波大厂在在竞争。 第一波是我们说的所有的手机厂。 第二波是所有的互联网络大场。 然后第三波是所有的模型公司。 所以，所以你知道因为这三波人最后都会希望去抢那个跟人交互的那个入口。 对吧？所以任何一家公司进这个品类的时候你都要回答说，今天你怎么能打得过前面你 2 3 这三类大场？ 所以你到底进还是不进？ 所以我们今天是不会到智能眼降那个品类去的。 对，但是人型机器人的话呢，今天它是还完全没有收敛。 对吧？然后我们今天在开发的，就是我们刚刚讲的狗这个形态的，其实我们的技术栈已经再向那个人形延伸了。 那我们希望说，到那个点，就是真的技术大家已经开始收敛的那那个点，我们有能力去挑战那样的市场。 当然那个挑战大概率就不是说用今天公司这种好像一个 组织的一部分去做。 可能就是从公司里边，成立一个更加独立的团队，去打那样的超级品类市场。 有可能。 有可能会 spin-off。 嗯，对，它其实 做得好，它才有 spin-off 的必要。 所以其实它有点像华为的秘密商业务里边长出来的手机业务一样的。 对吧？它其实变成一个深海之后，这个业务就完全独立的在自己跑。 然后到今天也跑得很成功。 去对你来说是一个必须要做的东西吗？还是你其实是观望的状态？ 我们觉得今天做一家做深海的公司来讲，这个其实不是我来就必须要做的。 对吧？而是今天当实际成熟的时候，我有能力做的时候，然后我们又友合适的 leader 去做这个事情的时候，我们觉得我们愿意去挑战的事情。 就它就是你们公司 也许可能的 宽广上的名字，但是今天其实他不确定。 嗯，我觉得今天其实你看华为，华为其实它的比商业务本质上是一大片的前海凑在一起的。 就它是很多很多的中小品类拼起来的。 然后它的手机业务其实是一个巨大的深海。 对吧？所以今天在一家公司里边，既有今天大片的浅海业务，又有一个深海的业务。 华为是前面的一个很好的榜样。 能做成他们那样其实需要非常好的底子，对吧？然后非常强的组织的能和技术能力。 然后我觉得我们今天朝那个方向在努力。 对，你得去试什么样的人？ 我们有商业化的能力，对吧？我们有场景化的产品能力，我们的技术能力现在也不是领，我们现在我觉得可能是个领点几，对吧？所以我们缺很好的技术人才，大家一起 来把我们的技术能力也做成一块很强的板的时候，那我觉得我们真的能够创造特别特别好的客户价值。 AI 芯片、具身智能、你分别愿意给的投入有多大呀？ 这个问题很难，你从好像一个——就是一个总数的角度来回答。对吧？我们还是回到就是说今天我们具体的问题是什么？ 然后，那么我们要解内几个问题，以及我们今天解这个问题我们需要一个什么样的投入？这样当讲的，就是我们大概率不会去训练这种万亿级别的模型，对吧？然后所以我们也不会去做那个级别的芯片。 Okay，当我们今天在训，就是我们说的，就是从一些神经网络这种百万到几百万参数到几千万参数的模型的时候，包括我们在探索去深，对吧？去深领域现在大家基准尝试是几十亿的模型，我们去这些模型的时候，然后我们匹配的，就是模型的训练的资源和芯片的资源，我觉得大概应该在就是几个亿的量级的。 对吧？然后至于 AI 的话，其实你很难说你的 AI 上的投入具体是多少？ 或者是—— 就是其实一定要算数的话，是可以算，就是比如我们有 200 多个人工智能工程师，对吧？人均的收入估计在大几十万的样子。所以，但这个算出来就很——对，就很无聊了，对吧？ 说说说你们的机器儿业务吧，具深的业务。 其实那——刚才说的是芯片。 对，刚才说了一些芯片，对吧？说了一些纯算的东西。那具机器人，其实我们把机器儿分成三个形态。 OK，二维的平面机器人。 就是今天的扫地机，割草机，对吧？包括永知机器儿它可以理解成 2.5 D 的机器人。这是第一阶段。然后第二阶段呢，就是今天三维空间里边的移动和交互的机器人。今天其实机器狗，对吧？宠物属于这个类型。第三阶段肯定就是最后的皇冠明珠了，就是操作的机器人，然后大概率几年来认为是一个人形的一个状态，对吧？那么所以今天 1 2 3 3 个阶段，那么其实我们在做你知道我们其实有一个 扫地机机器和割草机器人的业务。 我们在做第一阶段的业务，然后我们在开发第二阶段的产品。 我们在探索在千言预言，第三阶段人性的东西。 人性机器人会是你们一个很大的 battle 吗？ 今天我们的团队还是在一个就是预言和探索的状态。 因为，嗯，我们觉得今天这个领域的技术站还完全不收敛。 嗯，如果你看的话，嗯，大闹到底是什么样的状态，对吧？然后今天这个人的到底他的腰关节到底长什么样？你发现这些东西其实大家 solution 解决方案都还完全不同。 所以这个时候我们觉得可能还不是一个产品化的时候，它比较适合去预言。 而相反的话呢，今天你观察像狗这样的产品，就是二阶段的产品， 其实各家的形态都已经非常接近了。 嗯，所以从硬件上来讲，这个技术战已经比较收敛。 那么回到今天的模型阶段的话，大家的做法基本上也是非常的的一致，对吧？就是今天 Agent 的投在一个模型上面，所以我们觉得那这样的那个产品应该是可以进入产品化的阶段。 所以我们就从去年开始，就是有一个团队，我们在开发一条机器狗。 其实狗今天在我们的生活中到底扮演什么样的角色，提供什么样的价值？ 对吧？那当然今天狗是一个宠物，它给你提供情感的价值，但你发现狗最早进入家庭的时候，它其实起的是看家护院的作用，它提供的是功能价值。 对吧？那其实你发现今天的宠物狗很大程度上已经失去了当年的那个功能价值。 而我们自己做安防系统，那么我们在这个全球的几百万户期气家庭里面持续面对的一个问题就是说，你在家里边装满了安防的系统摄像头，对吧，然后包括报景的设备，但是今天如果有人闯入你家的时候，事实上你能做的事情是很少的。 那今天我们觉得当你有条看家狗，在今天有闯入者被这个安防系统侦测到了时候，这只狗可以去驱离这样的一个闯入者，实现了了一个用户价值的闭环。 对吧？就不仅是今天发现闯入者，而且实际上是能够驱离闯入者，让你家真的变得安全。 嗯，这样的一种产品听起来是不是有道理的？ 所以事实上我们去做用户研究也发现说，我们那些 77 的用户，然后大家今天有相当的比例愿意用相当的金额去买这样的一条家庭的看家狗。 换句话说这个产品其实在我们开始做的时候，我们确定它是能卖出去的，而且它是能卖出去很大量的量，因为它能创造特别明确的客户价值。 你听起来这个时代跟上一个时代你们做产品的方方面面也没有什么很大的区别，对吧？依然是定义市场的能力， 定义产品的能力。 我觉得任何一个时代，只要你叫产品公司，你就必须给客户创造价值。 不可能说你的产品对吧，技术特别先进，但是哦客户其实觉得它没有价值。 那这绝对不是一个好的产品公司。 所以我觉得我们这 30 多年从简单的产品，像充电宝那样的对吧，一直做到今天复杂的像看家狗这种产品。 公司的 我们信念非常的一贯，就是我们一定要做给客户创造真实价值的产品。 那为什么这个战略要从 前海转像深海呢？有转过型吗？ 我们还是说今天深不是今天这个品类有多复杂，而是代表这个品类的规模有多大。 对吧？换句话说，我们说所谓的深是这个品类的销售规模在 500 亿美金以上的。 这个其实它就像我们刚才举的德州扑克的例子。 实际上是你是这个德州扑克桌上每一住 盲住的大小。 所以换句话说就是我们说深海，就是盲注在几百万人民币对吧，到可能几千万人民币的市场的。 然后所谓深，就是一个盲注可能五个亿十个亿的市场。 所以在这样的市场里面，你发现其实即使是，对吧，就是有几百亿的钱甚至几百亿的钱，其实你可能打不了几注牌就会下去了。 所以那种市场我觉得其实跟—— 就我们说那个是深海和浅海的差别。 从这个角度的话，我们今天做的依然也是深海的市场。 你理解，对吧？即使是安防系统，像看家狗这种市场，它的整个市场规模大概率也不会超过 500 亿美金。 所以前海战略是依然延延的。 你回晋升海吗？ 其实你现在能看得到的两个未来确定性好出来的深海，一个就是我们刚刚讲的人型机器人。 对。 对吧？然后第二个呢其实我们觉得是智能眼降。 也就是个人交互终端的第——全新的这一种形态。 那今天人型眼降的话，我们觉得前面有一大堆公司。 这是智能眼降。 对，智能眼降。 还要我们觉得前面会有三波大厂在在竞争。 第一波是我们说的所有的手机厂。 第二波是所有的互联网络大场。 然后第三波是所有的模型公司。 所以，所以你知道因为这三波人最后都会希望去抢那个跟人交互的那个入口。 对吧？所以任何一家公司进这个品类的时候你都要回答说，今天你怎么能打得过前面你 2 3 这三类大场？ 所以你到底进还是不进？ 所以我们今天是不会到智能眼降那个品类去的。 对，但是人型机器人的话呢，今天它是还完全没有收敛。 对吧？然后我们今天在开发的，就是我们刚刚讲的狗这个形态的，其实我们的技术栈已经再向那个人形延伸了。 那我们希望说，到那个点，就是真的技术大家已经开始收敛的那那个点，我们有能力去挑战那样的市场。 当然那个挑战大概率就不是说用今天公司这种好像一个 组织的一部分去做。 可能就是从公司里边，成立一个更加独立的团队，去打那样的超级品类市场。 有可能。 有可能会 spin-off。 嗯，对，它其实 做得好，它才有 spin-off 的必要。 所以其实它有点像华为的秘密商业务里边长出来的手机业务一样的。 对吧？它其实变成一个深海之后，这个业务就完全独立的在自己跑。 然后到今天也跑得很成功。 去对你来说是一个必须要做的东西吗？还是你其实是观望的状态？ 我们觉得今天做一家做深海的公司来讲，这个其实不是我来就必须要做的。 对吧？而是今天当实际成熟的时候，我有能力做的时候，然后我们又友合适的 leader 去做这个事情的时候，我们觉得我们愿意去挑战的事情。 就它就是你们公司 也许可能的 宽广上的名字，但是今天其实他不确定。 嗯，我觉得今天其实你看华为，华为其实它的比商业务本质上是一大片的前海凑在一起的。 就它是很多很多的中小品类拼起来的。 然后它的手机业务其实是一个巨大的深海。 对吧？所以今天在一家公司里边，既有今天大片的浅海业务，又有一个深海的业务。 华为是前面的一个很好的榜样。 能做成他们那样其实需要非常好的底子，对吧？然后非常强的组织的能和技术能力。 然后我觉得我们今天朝那个方向在努力。 对，你得去试什么样的人？ 我们有商业化的能力，对吧？我们有场景化的产品能力，我们的技术能力现在也不是领，我们现在我觉得可能是个领点几，对吧？所以我们缺很好的技术人才，大家一起 来把我们的技术能力也做成一块很强的板的时候，那我觉得我们真的能够创造特别特别好的客户价值。 AI 芯片、具身智能、你分别愿意给的投入有多大呀？ 这个问题很难，你从好像一个——就是一个总数的角度来回答。对吧？我们还是回到就是说今天我们具体的问题是什么？ 然后，那么我们要解内几个问题，以及我们今天解这个问题我们需要一个什么样的投入？这样当讲的，就是我们大概率不会去训练这种万亿级别的模型，对吧？然后所以我们也不会去做那个级别的芯片。 然后，那么我们要解内几个问题，以及我们今天解这个问题我们需要一个什么样的投入？这样当讲的，就是我们大概率不会去训练这种万亿级别的模型，对吧？然后所以我们也不会去做那个级别的芯片。 Okay，当我们今天在训，就是我们说的，就是从一些神经网络这种百万到几百万参数到几千万参数的模型的时候，包括我们在探索去深，对吧？去深领域现在大家基准尝试是几十亿的模型，我们去这些模型的时候，然后我们匹配的，就是模型的训练的资源和芯片的资源，我觉得大概应该在就是几个亿的量级的。 对吧？然后至于 AI 的话，其实你很难说你的 AI 上的投入具体是多少？ 或者是—— 就是其实一定要算数的话，是可以算，就是比如我们有 200 多个人工智能工程师，对吧？人均的收入估计在大几十万的样子。所以，但这个算出来就很——对，就很无聊了，对吧？ 说说说你们的机器儿业务吧，具深的业务。 其实那——刚才说的是芯片。 对，刚才说了一些芯片，对吧？说了一些纯算的东西。那具机器人，其实我们把机器儿分成三个形态。 OK，二维的平面机器人。 就是今天的扫地机，割草机，对吧？包括永知机器儿它可以理解成 2.5 D 的机器人。这是第一阶段。然后第二阶段呢，就是今天三维空间里边的移动和交互的机器人。今天其实机器狗，对吧？宠物属于这个类型。第三阶段肯定就是最后的皇冠明珠了，就是操作的机器人，然后大概率几年来认为是一个人形的一个状态，对吧？那么所以今天 1 2 3 3 个阶段，那么其实我们在做你知道我们其实有一个 扫地机机器和割草机器人的业务。 我们在做第一阶段的业务，然后我们在开发第二阶段的产品。 我们在探索在千言预言，第三阶段人性的东西。 人性机器人会是你们一个很大的 battle 吗？ 今天我们的团队还是在一个就是预言和探索的状态。 因为，嗯，我们觉得今天这个领域的技术站还完全不收敛。 嗯，如果你看的话，嗯，大闹到底是什么样的状态，对吧？然后今天这个人的到底他的腰关节到底长什么样？你发现这些东西其实大家 solution 解决方案都还完全不同。 所以这个时候我们觉得可能还不是一个产品化的时候，它比较适合去预言。 而相反的话呢，今天你观察像狗这样的产品，就是二阶段的产品， 其实各家的形态都已经非常接近了。 嗯，所以从硬件上来讲，这个技术战已经比较收敛。 那么回到今天的模型阶段的话，大家的做法基本上也是非常的的一致，对吧？就是今天 Agent 的投在一个模型上面，所以我们觉得那这样的那个产品应该是可以进入产品化的阶段。 所以我们就从去年开始，就是有一个团队，我们在开发一条机器狗。 其实狗今天在我们的生活中到底扮演什么样的角色，提供什么样的价值？ 对吧？那当然今天狗是一个宠物，它给你提供情感的价值，但你发现狗最早进入家庭的时候，它其实起的是看家护院的作用，它提供的是功能价值。 对吧？那其实你发现今天的宠物狗很大程度上已经失去了当年的那个功能价值。 而我们自己做安防系统，那么我们在这个全球的几百万户期气家庭里面持续面对的一个问题就是说，你在家里边装满了安防的系统摄像头，对吧，然后包括报景的设备，但是今天如果有人闯入你家的时候，事实上你能做的事情是很少的。 那今天我们觉得当你有条看家狗，在今天有闯入者被这个安防系统侦测到了时候，这只狗可以去驱离这样的一个闯入者，实现了了一个用户价值的闭环。 对吧？就不仅是今天发现闯入者，而且实际上是能够驱离闯入者，让你家真的变得安全。 嗯，这样的一种产品听起来是不是有道理的？ 所以事实上我们去做用户研究也发现说，我们那些 77 的用户，然后大家今天有相当的比例愿意用相当的金额去买这样的一条家庭的看家狗。 换句话说这个产品其实在我们开始做的时候，我们确定它是能卖出去的，而且它是能卖出去很大量的量，因为它能创造特别明确的客户价值。 你听起来这个时代跟上一个时代你们做产品的方方面面也没有什么很大的区别，对吧？依然是定义市场的能力， 定义产品的能力。 我觉得任何一个时代，只要你叫产品公司，你就必须给客户创造价值。 不可能说你的产品对吧，技术特别先进，但是哦客户其实觉得它没有价值。 那这绝对不是一个好的产品公司。 所以我觉得我们这 30 多年从简单的产品，像充电宝那样的对吧，一直做到今天复杂的像看家狗这种产品。 公司的 我们信念非常的一贯，就是我们一定要做给客户创造真实价值的产品。 那为什么这个战略要从 前海转像深海呢？有转过型吗？ 我们还是说今天深不是今天这个品类有多复杂，而是代表这个品类的规模有多大。 对吧？换句话说，我们说所谓的深是这个品类的销售规模在 500 亿美金以上的。 这个其实它就像我们刚才举的德州扑克的例子。 实际上是你是这个德州扑克桌上每一住 盲住的大小。 所以换句话说就是我们说深海，就是盲注在几百万人民币对吧，到可能几千万人民币的市场的。 然后所谓深，就是一个盲注可能五个亿十个亿的市场。 所以在这样的市场里面，你发现其实即使是，对吧，就是有几百亿的钱甚至几百亿的钱，其实你可能打不了几注牌就会下去了。 所以那种市场我觉得其实跟—— 就我们说那个是深海和浅海的差别。 从这个角度的话，我们今天做的依然也是深海的市场。 你理解，对吧？即使是安防系统，像看家狗这种市场，它的整个市场规模大概率也不会超过 500 亿美金。 所以前海战略是依然延延的。 你回晋升海吗？ 其实你现在能看得到的两个未来确定性好出来的深海，一个就是我们刚刚讲的人型机器人。 对。 对吧？然后第二个呢其实我们觉得是智能眼降。 也就是个人交互终端的第——全新的这一种形态。 那今天人型眼降的话，我们觉得前面有一大堆公司。 这是智能眼降。 对，智能眼降。 还要我们觉得前面会有三波大厂在在竞争。 第一波是我们说的所有的手机厂。 第二波是所有的互联网络大场。 然后第三波是所有的模型公司。 所以，所以你知道因为这三波人最后都会希望去抢那个跟人交互的那个入口。 对吧？所以任何一家公司进这个品类的时候你都要回答说，今天你怎么能打得过前面你 2 3 这三类大场？ 所以你到底进还是不进？ 所以我们今天是不会到智能眼降那个品类去的。 对，但是人型机器人的话呢，今天它是还完全没有收敛。 对吧？然后我们今天在开发的，就是我们刚刚讲的狗这个形态的，其实我们的技术栈已经再向那个人形延伸了。 那我们希望说，到那个点，就是真的技术大家已经开始收敛的那那个点，我们有能力去挑战那样的市场。 当然那个挑战大概率就不是说用今天公司这种好像一个 组织的一部分去做。 可能就是从公司里边，成立一个更加独立的团队，去打那样的超级品类市场。 有可能。 有可能会 spin-off。 嗯，对，它其实 做得好，它才有 spin-off 的必要。 所以其实它有点像华为的秘密商业务里边长出来的手机业务一样的。 对吧？它其实变成一个深海之后，这个业务就完全独立的在自己跑。 然后到今天也跑得很成功。 去对你来说是一个必须要做的东西吗？还是你其实是观望的状态？ 我们觉得今天做一家做深海的公司来讲，这个其实不是我来就必须要做的。 对吧？而是今天当实际成熟的时候，我有能力做的时候，然后我们又友合适的 leader 去做这个事情的时候，我们觉得我们愿意去挑战的事情。 就它就是你们公司 也许可能的 宽广上的名字，但是今天其实他不确定。 嗯，我觉得今天其实你看华为，华为其实它的比商业务本质上是一大片的前海凑在一起的。 就它是很多很多的中小品类拼起来的。 然后它的手机业务其实是一个巨大的深海。 对吧？而是今天当实际成熟的时候，我有能力做的时候，然后我们又友合适的 leader 去做这个事情的时候，我们觉得我们愿意去挑战的事情。 就它就是你们公司 也许可能的 宽广上的名字，但是今天其实他不确定。 嗯，我觉得今天其实你看华为，华为其实它的比商业务本质上是一大片的前海凑在一起的。 就它是很多很多的中小品类拼起来的。 然后它的手机业务其实是一个巨大的深海。 对吧？所以今天在一家公司里边，既有今天大片的浅海业务，又有一个深海的业务。 华为是前面的一个很好的榜样。 能做成他们那样其实需要非常好的底子，对吧？然后非常强的组织的能和技术能力。 然后我觉得我们今天朝那个方向在努力。 对，你得去试什么样的人？ 我们有商业化的能力，对吧？我们有场景化的产品能力，我们的技术能力现在也不是领，我们现在我觉得可能是个领点几，对吧？所以我们缺很好的技术人才，大家一起 来把我们的技术能力也做成一块很强的板的时候，那我觉得我们真的能够创造特别特别好的客户价值。 AI 芯片、具身智能、你分别愿意给的投入有多大呀？ 这个问题很难，你从好像一个——就是一个总数的角度来回答。对吧？我们还是回到就是说今天我们具体的问题是什么？ 然后，那么我们要解内几个问题，以及我们今天解这个问题我们需要一个什么样的投入？这样当讲的，就是我们大概率不会去训练这种万亿级别的模型，对吧？然后所以我们也不会去做那个级别的芯片。 Okay，当我们今天在训，就是我们说的，就是从一些神经网络这种百万到几百万参数到几千万参数的模型的时候，包括我们在探索去深，对吧？去深领域现在大家基准尝试是几十亿的模型，我们去这些模型的时候，然后我们匹配的，就是模型的训练的资源和芯片的资源，我觉得大概应该在就是几个亿的量级的。 对吧？然后至于 AI 的话，其实你很难说你的 AI 上的投入具体是多少？ 或者是—— 就是其实一定要算数的话，是可以算，就是比如我们有 200 多个人工智能工程师，对吧？人均的收入估计在大几十万的样子。所以，但这个算出来就很——对，就很无聊了，对吧？ 说说说你们的机器儿业务吧，具深的业务。 其实那——刚才说的是芯片。 对，刚才说了一些芯片，对吧？说了一些纯算的东西。那具机器人，其实我们把机器儿分成三个形态。 OK，二维的平面机器人。 就是今天的扫地机，割草机，对吧？包括永知机器儿它可以理解成 2.5 D 的机器人。这是第一阶段。然后第二阶段呢，就是今天三维空间里边的移动和交互的机器人。今天其实机器狗，对吧？宠物属于这个类型。第三阶段肯定就是最后的皇冠明珠了，就是操作的机器人，然后大概率几年来认为是一个人形的一个状态，对吧？那么所以今天 1 2 3 3 个阶段，那么其实我们在做你知道我们其实有一个 扫地机机器和割草机器人的业务。 我们在做第一阶段的业务，然后我们在开发第二阶段的产品。 我们在探索在千言预言，第三阶段人性的东西。 人性机器人会是你们一个很大的 battle 吗？ 今天我们的团队还是在一个就是预言和探索的状态。 因为，嗯，我们觉得今天这个领域的技术站还完全不收敛。 嗯，如果你看的话，嗯，大闹到底是什么样的状态，对吧？然后今天这个人的到底他的腰关节到底长什么样？你发现这些东西其实大家 solution 解决方案都还完全不同。 所以这个时候我们觉得可能还不是一个产品化的时候，它比较适合去预言。 而相反的话呢，今天你观察像狗这样的产品，就是二阶段的产品， 其实各家的形态都已经非常接近了。 嗯，所以从硬件上来讲，这个技术战已经比较收敛。 那么回到今天的模型阶段的话，大家的做法基本上也是非常的的一致，对吧？就是今天 Agent 的投在一个模型上面，所以我们觉得那这样的那个产品应该是可以进入产品化的阶段。 所以我们就从去年开始，就是有一个团队，我们在开发一条机器狗。 其实狗今天在我们的生活中到底扮演什么样的角色，提供什么样的价值？ 对吧？那当然今天狗是一个宠物，它给你提供情感的价值，但你发现狗最早进入家庭的时候，它其实起的是看家护院的作用，它提供的是功能价值。 对吧？那其实你发现今天的宠物狗很大程度上已经失去了当年的那个功能价值。 而我们自己做安防系统，那么我们在这个全球的几百万户期气家庭里面持续面对的一个问题就是说，你在家里边装满了安防的系统摄像头，对吧，然后包括报景的设备，但是今天如果有人闯入你家的时候，事实上你能做的事情是很少的。 那今天我们觉得当你有条看家狗，在今天有闯入者被这个安防系统侦测到了时候，这只狗可以去驱离这样的一个闯入者，实现了了一个用户价值的闭环。 对吧？就不仅是今天发现闯入者，而且实际上是能够驱离闯入者，让你家真的变得安全。 嗯，这样的一种产品听起来是不是有道理的？ 所以事实上我们去做用户研究也发现说，我们那些 77 的用户，然后大家今天有相当的比例愿意用相当的金额去买这样的一条家庭的看家狗。 换句话说这个产品其实在我们开始做的时候，我们确定它是能卖出去的，而且它是能卖出去很大量的量，因为它能创造特别明确的客户价值。 你听起来这个时代跟上一个时代你们做产品的方方面面也没有什么很大的区别，对吧？依然是定义市场的能力， 定义产品的能力。 我觉得任何一个时代，只要你叫产品公司，你就必须给客户创造价值。 不可能说你的产品对吧，技术特别先进，但是哦客户其实觉得它没有价值。 那这绝对不是一个好的产品公司。 所以我觉得我们这 30 多年从简单的产品，像充电宝那样的对吧，一直做到今天复杂的像看家狗这种产品。 公司的 我们信念非常的一贯，就是我们一定要做给客户创造真实价值的产品。 那为什么这个战略要从 前海转像深海呢？有转过型吗？ 我们还是说今天深不是今天这个品类有多复杂，而是代表这个品类的规模有多大。 对吧？换句话说，我们说所谓的深是这个品类的销售规模在 500 亿美金以上的。 这个其实它就像我们刚才举的德州扑克的例子。 实际上是你是这个德州扑克桌上每一住 盲住的大小。 所以换句话说就是我们说深海，就是盲注在几百万人民币对吧，到可能几千万人民币的市场的。 然后所谓深，就是一个盲注可能五个亿十个亿的市场。 所以在这样的市场里面，你发现其实即使是，对吧，就是有几百亿的钱甚至几百亿的钱，其实你可能打不了几注牌就会下去了。 所以那种市场我觉得其实跟—— 就我们说那个是深海和浅海的差别。 从这个角度的话，我们今天做的依然也是深海的市场。 你理解，对吧？即使是安防系统，像看家狗这种市场，它的整个市场规模大概率也不会超过 500 亿美金。 所以前海战略是依然延延的。 你回晋升海吗？ 其实你现在能看得到的两个未来确定性好出来的深海，一个就是我们刚刚讲的人型机器人。 对。 对吧？然后第二个呢其实我们觉得是智能眼降。 也就是个人交互终端的第——全新的这一种形态。 那今天人型眼降的话，我们觉得前面有一大堆公司。 这是智能眼降。 对，智能眼降。 还要我们觉得前面会有三波大厂在在竞争。 第一波是我们说的所有的手机厂。 第二波是所有的互联网络大场。 然后第三波是所有的模型公司。 所以，所以你知道因为这三波人最后都会希望去抢那个跟人交互的那个入口。 对吧？所以任何一家公司进这个品类的时候你都要回答说，今天你怎么能打得过前面你 2 3 这三类大场？ 所以你到底进还是不进？ 所以我们今天是不会到智能眼降那个品类去的。
